    <li> The Panasonic Oral Irrigator is not meant to be a replacement for the home product, which has a large tank,enabling you to clean your entire tooth surfaces at one time. To do a very good job with the Panasonic you must refill its tank at least 2 times. The force of the cleaning drops as the reservoir is depleted. Therefore, you have to repeat the product on the last teeth done before the refill. In addition, you are warned not to use rechargeable batteries. Regular ones last about 20 minutes. If you are prepared for these limitations, the Oral Irrigator is much better than having nothing and will do its intended job.</li>

    <li> Just got the Panasonic Oral Irrigator and tried it out. I&#x27;ve never used one of these things before, so I can&#x27;t compare it to any other brands. What I mean by \\&quot;messy\\&quot; is that you&#x27;re going to get water everywhere. I got water all over the mirror, my shirt, the bathroom counter, the walls ... everywhere.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Design Flaw&lt;br /&gt;I believe the Panasonic Oral Irrigator suffers from a major design flaw: the power switch. It&#x27;s a locking switch, which means when you switch it to the \\&quot;on\\&quot; position, it stays on until you switch it off. So, you open your mouth, aim the irrigator, and flip the switch and water begins pulsating out of the nozzle. If you&#x27;re not expecting the ensuing mess, you might frantically try to turn it off again. But you&#x27;ve probably moved your finger away from the switch, so you have to find it again while simultaneously trying to manage the watery mess. A better design would have been a button like most blenders have that must be held down to keep the power on. Once you let go of the button, the power should cut off.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Update: It&#x27;s been a few months and since I&#x27;m now willing to say \\&quot;I like it\\&quot;, I&#x27;m upping my rating to four stars. With practice I&#x27;ve gotten better at handling the power switch, though I still accidentally spray a stray stream of water around the bathroom once in a while.</li>

    <li> I use the Waterpik Ultra Water Flosser at home to clean my teeth, but for cleaning my teeth at work after lunch, or for those times when I&#x27;m travelling, this Panasonic Oral Irrigator can&#x27;t be beat. The reservoir chamber is small, yes, but it can be easily refilled. I fill it twice for each use -- once for my lower teeth, and once for my upper. I also add a liquid \\&quot;soap\\&quot; to the reservoir -- a mixture of mouthwash and toothpaste (the toothpaste is liquified in the mouthwash). So far the irrigator has had no problem pumping this \\&quot;soap\\&quot;.    I purchased mine about 8 months ago and started by using two, AA alkaline batteries. The irrigator was good at removing food, but not perfect -- I had to use the Waterpik at home to get rid of the deeper stuff (including the plaque stuck just under my gumline).    Despite the warning against using rechargable batteries, I went ahead and starting using NiMH rechargeable AA batteries, but I noticed the unit wasn&#x27;t pumping as hard as with the alkaline, and so I had even more difficulty getting the daytime plaque removed.    I decided I needed more voltage. I purchased a lithium-ion 14500 battery (very close in size to a AA, but the nubby tab is reduced in height). That comes out of the charger at 4.0 volts. Couldn&#x27;t use two of those, so I made a dummy battery out of a #10 bolt, #10 washers, and fasteners (to keep the washers attached) and ground the dummy battery down to size. That made the irrigator too powerful, and I felt like my gums were being laser-cut away. Also, the lithium-ion battery started to fade after about 20 seconds (the power of the irrigator went down). To get the lithium-ion voltage down, I made another dummy battery but with two bolts connected to a central plastic tube and two diodes in parallel (taken from an old AC adapter) to reduce the voltage -- it connected the two bolts together. They reduced the voltage by 0.3 volts, and after trying again, the irrigator no longer felt like it was cutting my gums away, but the diodes would heat up, and the 14500 li-ion battery would fade as before, plus that battery would only get through two days of lunch cleaning before it needed recharging (I could use the NiMH batteries for two weeks without problem).    My recent attempt now is using PowerGenix NiZn rechargeable AA batteries. They are advertized at 1.6 volts, but I measured them out of the charger at 1.8 volts. Now the irrigator is at the same pumping power as the li-ion battery was with the diodes, but the NiZn batteries don&#x27;t fade, and no more hand-making dummy batteries.  Time will tell how long the NiZn batteries will last, but so far they are the best match with this oral irrigator.    Oh, I should mention the irrigator is also great for returning to work after a dental visit. On low setting, I use it to flush out all the old amalgam bits the dentist left in my mouth.    UPDATE 3/12/2011  The NiZN batteries, while performing well in the Panasonic irrigator, have resulted in a little too much pressure in the long term. The increased water pressure has forced an opening under my gumline between two front teeth causing bleeding. I&#x27;m now using one NiZN battery with a Duracell low-self discharge NiMH battery. This has reduced the pressure enough to stop cutting open the gumline in the one spot while giving enough pressure to effectively clean my teeth and gums. I think ultimately this irrigator would do better with a strength setting from 1-10 much like the Waterpik&#x27;s plug-in model, then I wouldn&#x27;t have to experiment so much with different battery combinations. Despite all of my high-voltage experiments with this irrigator, it has held up and continues to run fine.    Update 5/31/2011  I continue to use one PowerGenix NiZn battery with one Duracell NiMH battery for the best balance of power without tearing up my gums. In this setup, I can use this irrigator for a week in my lunchtime cleaning before having to switch out batteries. I keep another pair of NiZn/NiMH batteries ready to go, so I&#x27;m never without good power. At the end of the week, I take the used NiZn/NiMH battery pair home and recharge them (each in their own chargers, I have to use the special charger that came with the NiZN for the NiZN battery).    I also switched from a toothpaste/mouthwash cleaning solution to a Dove soap/mouthwash cleaning solution. The toothpaste has micro-fine silica, and I was afraid that in a pressure washer like this irrigator it would blast away enamel and dental work. It may have contributed to that pocket in my gumline when I was using two NiZn batteries in the irrigator.    I take a cheese grater with a turn handle and insert the bar of Dove soap into the grater, then turn the handle many times to produce soap bits that land in a large,  microwaveable measuring cup. Then I fill the measuring cup with mouthwash and microwave the mixture to heat it up. I take one of those bamboo sticks that are found in the kitchen accessory sections of department stores and stir the mixture until all soap bits are dissolved. I then pour that mixture back into the empty mouthwash container. For dispensing, I have a used, plastic, shrimp cocktail container that I fill and use to squirt the soap in the Waterpik irrigator for home. For work, I use an empty, see-through, one-dollar shampoo container as found at stores like Target or Walgreens (meant for travel kits) and squirt a little soap solution from that into the Panasonic irrigator.    Despite using these various soaps and different power levels, the Panasonic irrigator continues to run fine.</li>

    <li> It does everything I wanted as promised.  Easy to use, easy to put together and I love that it is portable and uses batteries.</li>
    <li> This is an offensively bad product. Beyond awful. Do not under any circumstances buy it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Oral irrigators are, at their most basic level, a small water reservoir, some form of electricity, and a thin nozzle to spray high pressure water between your teeth. This product seems carefully designed to be the worst irrigator possible.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Design flaws:&lt;br /&gt;1. For some unfathomable reason, this product is actually two moving pieces, you have to slide the two pieces apart in order to fill the water reservoir, but once you do, there is nothing keeping the two pieces apart. Which means that if you were to set the product down on the counter while it is full of water, the two pieces of it would slide back together, draining the entire reservoir onto the counter. That&#x27;s right, you can&#x27;t even set this product down without draining it completely&lt;br /&gt;2. In order to use it, you have to awkwardly hold the top and bottom halves of the product just far enough apart that they don&#x27;t collapse and spray water everywhere, but not so far apart that the hose inside the reservoir stops working&lt;br /&gt;3. The product is so long, heavy, and unwieldy that it is extremely difficult to aim inside your mouth&lt;br /&gt;4. The pressure is so high that if you miss your teeth (which you will a lot), it will hit the roof of your mouth, your tongue, and your uvula. This hurts, a LOT&lt;br /&gt;5. The power button is positioned such that it is nested between the two pieces and can&#x27;t be turned on or off without a lot of work. This is almost impossible to deactivate without looking, which means you have to take a high pressure water jet out of your mouth just to deactivate it&lt;br /&gt;6. The pressure is so high that unless you close your mouth entirely, it will spray everywhere. This product made a huge mess all over my counter, and the only way to prevent this was to get on my knees, kneel over the sink, and use it with my head in the sink bowl, which, due to the ridiculous length and shape, means that aiming the device was haphazard at best&lt;br /&gt;7. When I took the product out of it&#x27;s packaging, it smelled like moldy vomit. The stench was absolutely nauseating, and I had to spend 5 minutes scrubbing the outside and the inside of the product as best I could. I then drained three entire reservoirs worth of soapy water to try and clean out and rinse the tube inside, and even THEN, it still didn&#x27;t work. My mouth now smells like vomit, so thanks for that.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Basically, to use this product, you have to slide it open, fill the reservoir, close the reservoir lid, hold the product vertically by both the top and bottom halves, keep them separated, put your head in the sink bowl, activate the device&#x27;s power without even being able to see it, then awkwardly spray the product around inside your mouth, frequently hitting and damaging the roof of your mouth and your uvula, occasionally choking and gagging when the high pressure spray goes down your throat, and having water occasionally spray everywhere.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Now, the most important question is: once you get past the offensively bad design, does the product actually work? NO. It does not. I have several troublesome teeth where food frequently gets caught, and as an experiment, I used three full reservoirs worth of water trying to clean these trouble spots. Then I took dental floss and checked the result. The irrigator did absolutely nothing.     <li> I&#x27;ve owned three of the Panasonic oral irrigators. The first two units I owned were the predecessor model with the darker blue color. Both the old and new have some great features: A powerful motorized pump and a nicely sized water resevoir you can collapse for travel. Even the telescoping nozel can be removed and stowed away. Very cool. The current model has improved on the previous design. The resevoir is larger. This makes it possible to clean out your whole mouth by only filling the resevoir once.      One design flaw with both models is the hinged door on the resevoir. You swing it open to add water. However, I lost the hinge door on my first two units about a year. After a while, the plastic pegs that hold it in place weaken and the door comes loose. I expect the same thing to eventually happen with the unit I just bought since it is built the same way. Another way the unit can fail is the motor. On my first unit, the motor progressively lost power after a year or so of daily use.     Overall, the Panasonic irrigators are an effective tool for keeping teeth clean and I will continue to buy them. I once owned a Waterpik model and it was inferior to the Panasonic: A smaller resevoir, you could not collapse it for travel, and the water flow was weak. Panasonic offers a superior product.    As an aside, my dentist told me to continue to floss. The irrigators can remove large stuff stuck between teeth, but are not as effective at removing food that sits below the gum line.</li>

    <li> I suppose that any product as dreadfully named as the Panasonic EW-DJ10-A is a product that&#x27;s likely to perform its intended task with single minded efficiency. Namely, it holds a respectable amount of water, shoots said water into one&#x27;s mouth, and in the process dislodges food particles.  So what more could one want in an oral irrigator?  Well, a little more power would be nice.  I also own a hydrofloss irrigator, and this little chap simply doesn&#x27;t compare in the pressure department.  It&#x27;s not bad at all, but it just doesn&#x27;t have the kick that the hydrofloss sports.     <li> I&#x27;ve been using a Braun-Oral B stationary water pick since 1999 and I&#x27;m totally satisfied with it. Unfortunately it was recently broken down after so many years of service. I wanted to buy it again, but the model was discontinued, perhaps because it was so durable and they couldn&#x27;t make a good sale out of it. After reading many reviews, I decided to buy this Panasonic portable water pick, keeping in mind that it has a disadvantage of being &#x27;bulky&#x27;, especially for my kids. I don&#x27;t mind this, because I&#x27;ve already ordered a WaterPick WP100 for them, as they are now a bit more grown up and stay on a different floor in our house.      The product arrived on time in a thorough packaging, though I believe the high quality of its plastic would not require such a thoroughness. Being prepared to see a bulky thing, I was not very surprised with its modest size when it was in the package. It was just 6.5 inches high. However, I admit that when I expanded it for use, I said \\&quot;wow\\&quot;. It became ~1.5 times higher, which is 8.5 inches. I&#x27;m now sure that my 8-year-old son wouldn&#x27;t love holding it in his hand. However, it should be OK for my daughter, who is only 6.3 feet height, because it is just right with me, who is not too much higher (I&#x27;m an Asian guy of medium size).     What I&#x27;m surprised most with this product is that its pulsated stream of water was just of the right size and power, the same like of the Braun-Oral B I used before. The only difference is that with Braun-Oral B I had the ability to adjust the power step-less. However, I always had to watch the dial to stay at the right low and high points that I later found other family members of mine also use. With Panasonic, the water is just right for normal cleaning when I turn it on, at the first speed. At the second speed, it is also just right for clearing of more &#x27;stubborn&#x27; debris. I can imagine how much research had the Panasonic engineers done to find out this point. Still, this is not everything. I must say that every detail of this product shows its quality and rationality. The pick is turnable so that you can adjust it at your right angle, but it sits tight enough to not rotate spontaneously. The hose inside the tank made the unit usable at any angle. The power switch and battery lid are very well water-proof. The lid of the tank is also very good. Once I rush to meetings and just throw the unit in my drawer without discharging redundant water. In the car, I realized that there were some important paper together with it, and I rushed back just to find out that nothing happened. There was no water leaking.    All in one, this portable water pick is paying off for its value of money. I would recommend it not only for travel, but also for use at home. It can even replace the stationary version, because thanks to the right size of its tank, you don&#x27;t have to refill it many times during one use.</li>

I wish it could be more clear but the file size has to be under 100 MB.    There are several pros and cons.    Pros.  1. It is very strong, the battery that came with it is manufactured by Panasonic as well, it is disposable battery, and the manual recommends no rechargeable battery.  2. The level 2 is even stronger than level 10 in the corded water flosser \\&quot;Waterpik WP-660 Aquarius Professional Water Flosser\\&quot;. I bought that for my mom, and I feel that is not as powerful as this one.  3. It removes all the things between your teeth perfectly.  4. Portable and looks very elegant, and can be carry everywhere, it can be smaller too by inserting the irrigator into the tank.  5. Consider this as an investment, it saves money compared to using the regular flosser. Still, the battery is an expense as well.    Cons.  1. The top where you insert battery is hard to open and close for the first time users. But it is understandable because it helps to prevent water from flowing in.  2. The tank cannot be detached from the irrigator, and the tank only has a little hole where you put water. This means it is hard to wipe the water inside using a paper. This can be problematic because if water is there for a period of time and you did not remove it, there can be mold as mentioned by other reviewers.  3. It only comes with one pick. The corded waterpik had all different types of pick for different use.  4. The tank is small and not enough for a thorough mouth clean.    I will put more updates if people find this helpful. Again, bear with me for my inexperience in making video review, I have to hold it one-handed.</li>

The first time I used this irrigator, I had fluid all over the sink and counter because I was not used to using it.  Over time, I finally adapted and was able to contain the fluid to the sink.  There are two speeds you can use.  The switch is easy to maneuver- slow, high, and off.  The tip easily rotates depending on what area of the mouth you are irrigating.  After using this for two weeks, I still cannot irrigate my mouth on one chamber-full of fluid because I am too slow.  I am sure over time I will become faster and more accurate.     <li> The directions say don&#x27;t use rechargeable batteries, and they mean it. Rechargeable NiMH (Nickel metal hydride) batteries put out 1.2 volts per cell, compared to 1.5 volts for alkaline throwaways. With the rechargeables, the irrigator does pump water, but it&#x27;s too weak to do much good. I&#x27;m getting about 15 to 20 uses out of a set of throwaways. At that point, the irrigator is still working but getting pretty weak. (The batteries still have enough juice to power a LED flashlight.) I think Panasonic could add a voltage regulator circuit that would enable you to use rechargeables and would extend the life of throwaways. That would increase the price, no doubt.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Aside from the need to use throwaway batteries, I&#x27;m satisfied with this item.</li>

    <li> After years of neglect I have been diagnosed with gum disease. I had a root scaling and planing and continued with my bad habits thinking that the problem will disappear. It is very maneuverable, much more that the Waterpik (limited by the hose lenght and rigidness). It is very easy to clean, as it comes apart very easily, and it has a very short water circuit (with the Waterpik I would always run it for a few seconds before using it because there was stale and stinky water inside the system - oh, the junk that took out of the Waterpik once!!! imagine that inside your mouth!!!). The reservoir is good size - could be a little bigger but it would defeat the portability purpose, so it&#x27;s not bad - just fill it once more! It has 2 power settings, and the water pressure is adequate, not only for a battery operated unit, but for a water flosser in general. Some people complain that the Waterpik (which is more powerful than this one) is not powerful enough. Well, unless you are trying to unglue your gums or dislocate your teeth or clean your bathroom with it, this is more than enough. I am a firm believer that the irrigator has to be used in conjunction with other means of oral hygiene, and not replace them entirely. So I floss, use the brushpicks and the irrigator (very often with a distilled water / hydrogen peroxide mix).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Most of the above I used religiously after every single meal. I became almost obsessed with the hygiene to the point that I would avoid snacking if I knew I couldn&#x27;t clean my teeth afterwards. But it all paid off! Now it&#x27;s 3 years later and I am free of any gum disease, and all my pockets are 3mm now. I still continue to employ most of the above techniques, and this Panasonic is an integral part of it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All in all, this is a highly recommended item!it for a few seconds before using it because there was stale and stinky water inside the system - oh, the junk that took out of the Waterpik once!!! imagine that inside your mouth!!!).     <li> I&#x27;ve used the Panasonic Oral Irrigator for about 10 years.  [[ASIN:B0027LSEPS Panasonic Oral Irrigator EW-DJ10-A]].  I used several Water Pik products before moving to Panasonic, but I stopped using Water Pik products because they turned into junk about 15 years ago.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;To extend the life of this Panasonic unit by 2 or 3 times, pull it apart right away after each time you use it.  That is, pull off the water resevoir, open up the plug where you fill it with water, and store the resevoir upside down, so it dries.  Then turn the rest of the unit on and let all the water squirt out of the body and pump.  Try to get out as much as you can.  Then store the unit that way until you&#x27;re ready to use it again. Don&#x27;t reassemble it again until you&#x27;re ready to use it again.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;A little vaseline or similar lubricant on the O rings every few months is also a good idea.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And one other thing, even drying it out the way I suggest, mold still tends to grow inside the resevoir and in several places on the outside -- even on the Jet Pik handle.  Use an old toothbrush and some Peroxide to clean or wipe off any mold you see.  You can also soak the Jet Pick in Peroxide overnight to clean it out and help keep mold from growing in there.  And if you have a piece of wire or small enough brush to fit into/through the Jet Pik, use that too, to help clean it out.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Follow these tips and you&#x27;ll have a cleaner Water Pik that lasts a lot longer.  Good luck.  It&#x27;s a great product!</li>

    <li> About 90% of the oral irrigators on the market are designed for families sharing family-size bathrooms.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For those of us living alone - about 28% of all Americans - finding a place on the counter-tops of our typically tiny bathrooms for these behemoths is a major challenge. Once you deal with the mold problem in the bottom of the reservoir, all kinds of nooks and crannies need to be cleaned.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Every time I change the batteries - about every 3 weeks - I remove the reservoir (don&#x27;t be afraid to pull really hard) and soak it in bleach.  I clean all the crevices with toothpicks and Q-tips. Takes me about 15 min.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Have also learned that the Panasonic must be kept vertically at all times since the battery section is not water tight.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So - if you angle the unit - for example, while trying to get to the backs of your teeth, you will be compromising the life of the batteries.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Learned after a couple of trips to remove the batteries completely - unless you can figure out a way to keep it vertical the entire time you are in transit.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The two Panasonics I&#x27;ve had died after about one year.  The pumps just stop working.  But in each case, the Warranty has been honored.  Keep the box!&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So.... the Panasonic is the best of the Travel irrigators currently available.
